> **MissionImpossible said:**
> Yes, they are very important ethical and ideological issues to consider and keep faithful to.


 I got a little bit of info from the openSuse website on Restricted_Formats & Legal Issues and hope others can expand a bit more on it.

Here is what I think is relevant for consideration in this current discussion:

DVD Video

Legal Issues

DVDs are encoded in mpeg2-video, for which there is no support in the current SUSE Linux OSS release, due to the mpeg2 format being patent-encumbered by its owners, Moving Picture Experts Group (MPEG).**The patent holder is unwilling to offer an unrestricted patent grant, as required by the GPL.**
[http://en.opensuse.org/GNU_General_Public_License](http://en.opensuse.org/GNU_General_Public_License)

In addition region-coded DVDs are encrypted with the Content Scrambling System (CSS).

There is an opensource project called libdvdcss that bypasses this encryption. Though the encryption is weak, using any method or device to bypass this is classed as a 'circumvention device' in countries such as USA & Australia and is illegal.....

Possible Solutions

In some countries it is legal to backup your own DVD. .....


MP3

Legal Issues

Even though MPEG Audio Layer 3 (MP3) is an ISO standard it is not a free and open standard and is covered by numerous patents. **It is not legal to distribute unlicensed MP3 Decoders and Encoders in most countries. [COLOR="Red"]And if you would ship an mp3 decoder, you can not link it from GPL software, because this would cause a GPL violation (because of its additional restrictions)**[/COLOR]

Possible Solutions

Novell has a contract which allows it to provide mp3 support via the RealPlayer. It can be used via amaroK or banshee. RealPlayer is not open source, so you have to add the [http://download.opensuse.org/distribution/10.2/repo/non-oss/suse/](http://download.opensuse.org/distribution/10.2/repo/non-oss/suse/) to your YaST sources and install either banshee-helix or RealPlayer to get mp3 playback support in these applications.

You can use the Fluendo MP3 decoder which is a fully licensed yet free GStreamer plugin for MP3....

As an alternative, enocode your audio files in Ogg Vorbis, FLAC, Speex, or other such freely used and available audio codecs.

**Please note, Thomson, which holds the patents for mp3 and other formats, states [1] that "...no license is needed for private, non-commercial activities (e.g., home-entertainment, receiving broadcasts and creating a personal music library), not generating revenue or other consideration of any kind or for entities with associated annual gross revenue less than US$ 100 000.00." This may also be confirmed on the Thomson licensing page [2]....**


NTFS

Legal Issues

NTFS was invented by Microsoft. At the moment there isn't any known patent which would be potentially violated by any existing open source NTFS code, in any country. In fact, in some countries Microsoft must pay multi million dollar fines and is obliged to publish interoperability documents due to anti-competitive practices.

[COLOR="Red"]**Many support the use of FAT/FAT32 to share files between Windows and Linux on a single system. As Microsoft's patent on FAT has been upheld as of January 2006, long filenames on FAT partitions may not be used, as they are patent-encumbered.**[/COLOR]


Possible Solutions

It is preferable to use a file system natively supported by Linux, such as ext3 or reiserfs. F/LOSS software exists for windows which will make use of various Linux-supported and originating file systems. To share these drives, format them using an available and eligible supported file system.

Ext2 IFS is a "freeware" (not free software) installable ext2 file system for Windows. It integrates with the Windows kernel thereby providing access to files on ext2 (and ext3) partitions seamlessly to all applications.

explore2fs supports ext2 and ext3 from within Windows.

Samba enables your system to access file shares on networked Windows systems, and to act as a server for Windows systems.

If you wish to mount an NTFS partition in Linux, you need to install ntfs-3g and fuse, for Suse v10.x, x<3. 

NVIDIA Graphics Drivers

Legal Issues

**The NVIDIA graphics drivers are proprietary and many kernel developers consider this driver to violate the GPL license of the kernel.** 

[http://en.opensuse.org/Restricted_Formats](http://en.opensuse.org/Restricted_Formats)

Yeh but its from opensuse who's main sponsor is a Microsoft turncoat.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-10-09
> **mahiyar said:**
> Now I know why PCLinux OS is popular. I bet in their forums, members are treated with more respect, threads are not merged arbitrarily, if the condition to post is the the non-existence of a topic, there would be no new posts. Posts are questions to help members, answer their queries, and my question (what can PClos do that Ubuntu cannot) still remains unanswered, thanks to the thread merger. Threads aren't merged arbitrarily here either.

There are only two reasons we'd merge a thread:
1. It's a discussion (not technical support) thread that already exists in another thread
2. It's a technical support thread that already exists *for the same user*. In other words, it's one person posting multiple threads about the same problem.

If you see any situation other than that, report it.

Whereas here, people are more exposed to the options, as this forum is already so active, and the friendly atmosphere makes people feel comfortable enough to tinker, knowing either the knowledge they need is already in a thread somewhere, or that their thread will most likely be answered.

> Novell has also created or is among the top sponsors of projects such as the Linux Kernel, GCC, OpenOffice.org, KDE, GNOME, Tomboy, F-Spot, Banshee, Beagle, (K)NetworkManager, Kickoff, Evolution, XEN, Xgl, and Compiz etc. Are you refusing to use any of those as well? Since they all have substantial amounts of Novell code.


[http://en.opensuse.org/Novell_Supported_Projects](http://en.opensuse.org/Novell_Supported_Projects)

### Post by sstusick on 2007-10-12

Also, you can't beat the great community Ubuntu has! And for that, Ubuntu is a keeper.

### Post by SuperMike on 2007-10-14
I don't see why people switch to PCLinuxOS over Ubuntu. I mean, think about Ubuntu:

* Backed by a billionaire.

* Is made by scores of developers both at Canonical and with the Debian team, rather than a handful.

* Is created in a country outside of USA patent law.

* It doesn't have a philosophy of dissing other distros posted visibly on their site like PCLinuxOS does.

* Mails you free CDs and low cost purchase options.

* Last I checked, has $25-per-call tech support (although they want you to do an annual contract).

* Has a far larger volume of users than other distros -- just look at the enormous volume of posts here in ubuntuforums.org compared with every other distro on the planet.

* Has a fantastic, top-quality bug tracking site that is very active.

* Has a good mix of noobs all the way to pros as far as expertise. That mix is extremely healthy for Ubuntu's survival and both noobs and pros are encouraged by Canonical.

* Has revolutionized how Linux can be user-friendly. Before Ubuntu, I couldn't even think of installing it on my relative's PCs, but now I can.


Plain and simple, man, Ubuntu gets you laid. Okay, just kidding, but I just don't see all the wild advantages of PCLinuxOS over Ubuntu.

### Post by anshuljain on 2007-10-20
I run PCLinuxOS on my desktop and Ubuntu Feisty on my laptop...both entirely different DE's  (GNOME and KDE). I find both of them to be equally good, although I give PCLinuxOS an edge as I'm more of a KDE fan.

Advantages of PCLinuxOS:
-Works out of the box, no configuation needed. 
-Great collection of ready to use apps...Amarok, K3B, Kopete, X-Chat, Kaffeine/KMPlayer etc. 
-Incredibly stable, for a KDE distro
-Rolling updates which are pushed only after a thorough test
-Drakconfig control center (actual credit: Mandriva)

Advantages of Ubuntu
-Fantastically simple to use 
-Codec and Restricted driver installation, a treat which every distro should emulate (Fedora has something similar called codec-buddy lined for their 8 release)
-Great community. Yes, I can find all answers here. Kudos to the Ubuntu community!
-Automatic update manager. Many distros lack this most basic and easy tool. 

I actually did find a "common ground" distro between Ubuntu and PCLinuxOS and that happens to be Mandriva 2008. It has the best of both..and really soli.d But YMMV.

I'd say this guys, its Microshaft Windoze thats the enemy and not Ubuntu and PCLinuxOS against each other. Go Linux!!

-Anshul

---

### Post by kbozen on 2007-10-23
from DistroWatch Weekly, Issue 225, 22 October 2007

The Page Hit Ranking experiment


[http://distrowatch.com/weekly.php?issue=20071022#experiment](http://distrowatch.com/weekly.php?issue=20071022#experiment)



As many of you noticed, the Page Hit Ranking table disappeared from the front page of DistroWatch on Wednesday last week. This wasn't a glitch and it wasn't done in order to develop a better ranking system - it was simply a 24-hour experiment to determine how the absence of the table would affect the number of hits each distribution page received. The main reason for this experiment was a continuous string of emails and forum posts from readers who suspect foul play due to the fact that a relatively little-known distribution now occupies the top spot in the Page Hit Ranking table. Could it be that there are a few automatic web bots that load the PCLinuxOS page in regular intervals and thus artificially inflate the number of page views?

[]

By removing the Page Hit Ranking table from the front page of DistroWatch, the PCLinuxOS page received only 684 unique hits, which is about 18% of the number of visits it received during the previous day and the following day. Of these 684 hits, only 8.5% of the visitors' user agent string reported PCLinuxOS as the operating system used. If we remove from the column those distributions that happened to feature in the front-page news on that particular day (i.e. StartCom Linux, PAIPIX, elpicx and VectorLinux), PCLinuxOS would still rank fairly high - at number 5, just behind Ubuntu, openSUSE, Fedora and Mandriva Linux).

---

### Post by verb3k on 2007-10-23

This bad distro will perish because of its dirty tricks.

### Post by Perpetual on 2007-10-23
Not a PCLOS fanboy but in all fairness, the rest of the article should be noted...

[i]It is rather obvious that the reason for the high number of hits on the PCLinuxOS page is nothing other than sheer curiosity of those DistroWatch readers who are new to the Linux world and who find it surprising that a relatively unknown distribution is at the number one spot. In order to learn more, they click on the link which takes them to the PCLinuxOS page. Until this curiosity is satisfied and PCLinuxOS becomes a more mainstream distribution, we are unlikely to see a huge drop in the number of visits on the PCLinuxOS page.

Several readers suggested that this was a "problem that needed fixing". If PCLinuxOS continued to lead the ranking, they argued, the table would become meaningless as it would no longer represent the relative popularity of distributions. While this is certainly true, we should also remember that PCLinuxOS did not get to the number one spot by means of a random placement, but by slow, continuous rise in interest and curiosity generated by you - the DistroWatch reader. If a distribution is good enough to get there, then be it! While PCLinuxOS might be nowhere near as popular and widely-used as Ubuntu or openSUSE, it certainly is an excellent project which has a growing and satisfied user base.

Finally, let's not forget one other important thing: once you reach the number one spot in anything, there is only one way to go from there - down ;-)[/i]

### Post by beeldings on 2007-10-28
I've been an Ubuntu user since "Warty Warthog" and while Ubuntu is a solid operating system, I was disappointed with Gutsy Gibbon. To me, Gutsy feels like it is still a beta release and doesn't meet the usual quality of previous releases. As I pointed out in another thread, Gutsy does introduce some worthwhile features, but on the whole there was a lot of breakage, stability issues, and too many little inconviences that prevented it from being the definitive Ubuntu.

After an ungratifying performance this release, I decided to nuke Gutsy and go distro-shopping. I revisited some old favorites like Fedora and openSuSE, and I tried some not-so-popular (according to DistroWatch) releases such as Sabayon Linux. While each distribution has it's own niche, none of them felt like a complete distribution like Ubuntu did pre-Gutsy Gibbon. With that in mind, I scoured DistroWatch again and settled on a distribution that I've ignored simply because it was based on Mandrake (and I had problems with Mandrake years ago): PCLinuxOS.

I downloaded the GNOME remaster of PCLinuxOS last night. While it is not as polished as the KDE release, compared to Ubuntu it is still a very nice looking release. The installation took less than 20 minutes and all of my hardware, including sound, was correctly detected and set-up. Like Ubuntu, I still had to download and compile the correct driver for my printer (HP LaserJet 1020), but with that done my printer was as easy to set-up with PCLOS as it was with Ubuntu. PCLOS does not include a restricted driver manager like Ubuntu, but installation of my NVIDIA driver was as simple as loading Synaptic and choosing the appropriate package. PCLOS includes Flash, Java, and several media codecs out of the box whereas one has to use a tool like Automatix to install such necessities on Ubuntu.

One issue that most, if not all Ubuntu users are concerned with is performance, and how PCLOS compares to Ubuntu. On my system (A64 3200+, GF7800GS, 2Gb DDR) a stock install of PCLOS outperformed my tweaked Ubuntu installation. I haven't tested in-game performance, but both and log-in speeds were faster than Ubuntu. I also noticed that WoW installed faster on PCLOS than it did on Ubuntu (using CrossOver Standard 6.2).

If there's one thing that I don't like about PCLOS it is that the package repository does not include as many packages as Ubuntu. I'm still new to PCLOS but I believe that this can be corrected by using additional repositories. I also noticed that both Synaptic and apt-get are slower than there Ubuntu counterparts. I'm not sure whether this is because PCLOS is an RPM distro and Ubuntu is Debian based, but those two applications are not as fast as they are on Ubuntu.

In short, I am thoroughly impressed with PCLOS. If the PCLOS dev can improve upon an already solid foundation, I think it may overtake Ubuntu as the premier Linux distribution. For my personal use, I'm done with Ubuntu for now. Unless Hardy Heron can excel where Gutsy failed, I'll stick with PCLOS.

---

### Post by ebozzz on 2007-10-28
> **beeldings said:**
> PCLOS includes Flash, Java, and several media codecs out of the box whereas one has to use a tool like Automatix to install such necessities on Ubuntu.

This is not correct. You do **not** need to use Automatix to install those multimedia components. In fact, in about the same amount of time that it would take you to install them using Automatix you could have all of that working by using the tools already provided in Ubuntu. 

I tried PCLOS and it is a good distro. It wasn't good enough to make me switch. My graphics did not configure properly, sound and also I was never able to get the wireless to connect to any network. It did detect my card. It just would not connect. 

Graphics and WiFi worked immediately in Gutsy. I did have a problem with my sound card not being detected but all I had to do was download the correct linux-backports-modules with Synaptic and I am grooving! PCLOS is good but is the best that I have found for my needs thus far. Good luck with it. I do hope that it works out for you.

### Post by ledsled on 2007-10-29
The PCLOS repos are updated constantly. Because it's a rolling release system, all security updates and bug fixes are part of the standard update procedure in Synaptic There are now over 500MB of updates to a standard install. Which is very large and takes nearly as long as the initial install itself. Because of this, they are planning a new release, soon, with all the updates included. 
The speed of the repos is very fast, The mirror that I use, runs at an average of 500kbps. On my buddy's setup it's averaging  around 800kbps.
I really like the rolling release system because it means that all of my packages are the latest version without having to break my install while trying to upgrade  or completely re-installing every 6 months.

---

### Post by odysseusjak on 2007-10-29
I'm a HUGE PCLOS fan.  Been with them since around .92.  It did everything I needed it to without a lot of going to the terminal (my only gripe about Ubuntu, really). However, the base system lasted a number of years in the previous versions so all that was needed was to use Synaptic to update it.

And yes, the devs are all over the forums.  It is amazing who quickly stuff gets fixed or added to the repos.

### Post by shane2peru on 2007-10-29

### Post by ledsled on 2007-10-29
> **shane2peru said:**
> +1 - that is an interesting concept, then virtually there wouldn't be an upgrade of the system to the next version?  Am I understanding that correctly?  That is a very interesting concept.  
Oh, I remembered to part of that article had to do with the bugs, meaning there isn't a launchpad bug report system.  In other words there isn't a set place to report bugs so that they can be fixed by the actual developers.  In other words, the system is used on a wider range of machines than what the developers have on hand, and it may present a bug or issue with drivers that they may not know about, or run into.  So how would that be handled?  Forums are great, but I have run come to the end of the line without help on forums many times.  Again correct me if I'm wrong here, because I certainly don't know. Shane
Yes, that is correct. The devs are active on the forums including Texstar himself. Most problems get fixed very  fast.
When they upgraded the gcc to 4.x in the 2007 version, a re-install was necessary, but they are predicting 3 years until the next re-base.
The software in the repos is pretty up-to-date. I'm running a 2.6.22.8 32-bit kernel that is optimized for my  AMD 64 processor.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-11-04
**Question:**
Why is Ubuntu on 2nd place in DistroWatch?

**Answer:**
People have clicked on PCLinuxOS's DistroWatch page more times than they've clicked on Ubuntu's DistroWatch page.

If you want to take that to mean PCLinuxOS "fanboys" have "cheated" by clicking too many times on the PCLinuxOS page, then that's your right. If you want to take it to mean PCLinuxOS is getting more buzz than Ubuntu, that's your right as well.

---

### Post by Redlance on 2007-11-05
ok gonna post my useless 2 cents here.
one thing pclinuxos is above ubuntu in ranking on distrowatch. big deal i say. for these reasons.
1) its all still linux people.
2) look at pclinuxos rank now add ubuntu,kubunt,xubuntu, and edubuntu together. wow that rank there clearly blows away any hope of pclinux being number one.
3) all distros that follow the OSS/gpl path help each other. we learn from others. we share. we all benefit.

That being said. there alot of good distros and also poorly implemented distro's but they all contribute good for the whole. we learn new things and learn what is bad from all distro's that is the true power of linux. that is what sends shivers down balmer's spine. we are growing better and faster than windows can. soon the the difference between propietary software and oss will be so markedly different in stages of advancement that people will realize how trapped and decadant closed source is. especially in the future with the hybridization of the network being truly integrated into an os. Soon the flavor of OS will become less important but the ability for an OS to adapt to changing landscape of the networked world will decide the choice of OS. I enjoy linux  not because i hate ms or have some religious zealotry or fanboyisms.. I enjoy it because its like watching in wonder how adept your child has become. its learning and growing and its free as in creativity. (not as in beer)
:guitar:

gksu gnome-control-center

### Post by mahiyar on 2007-11-10
But the gnome control panel is a part of gutsy install, you will find it in system>preferences>control centre

---

### Post by Perpetual on 2007-11-10
> **mahiyar said:**
> But the gnome control panel is a part of gutsy install, you will find it in system>preferences>control centre

It has to be added to the menu as it's not in the menu by default.  System>Preferences>Main Menu and add it.

It however is nothing like the Mandriva Control Center that is used by PCLOS.  MCC is used more as a 'Control Panel' for more in depth system settings while GCC adds nothing more than what is already in the Gnome Menus.  It is just a more organized GUI.

### Post by adamklempner on 2007-11-17
> **Turin Turambar said:**
> Hey, sorry for my ignorance, but I'll have to ask something.
I wasn't into Linux world for quite some time...

What is so special about Pclinuxos? I tried to read this forum but I couldn't find an answer. Many people say it's good... but what? What does PClinuxos have that Ubuntu does not, in terms of programs, stability...? :confused:

PCLOS has the best implementation of KDE in any of the "ready made" distro's that I have  used recently (Linspire/Kubuntu - Gutsy/OpenSUSE/Mint - older KDE one).  The most noticeable things are speed and and the integration of Compiz-fuzion.  PCLinuxOS really feels faster on my computer (AthlonXP 3000+, 1 GB) than the other distros I listed.   Simple things like web browsing with Firefox were MUCH snappier.  I don't know why but PCLOS is the only one in which Firefox felt as fast as it is on XP.  Boot up is faster also.

I personally think the default KDE theme of PCLOS is ugly and too XP like, but I always change that anyway.

I also like the PCLOS repository a little better than the *buntu ones.  It is a lot smaller but it seems to have all of the apps that I need, and a bunch that I couldn't get with Kubuntu (a bunch of the video editing stuff that finally got added with Gutsy).  Also, I like how their repo is constantly doing rolling updates so you don't have to wait half a year to get new programs.   There is also a package suggestion forum that they seem to pay good attention to.

The PCLOS forum, however, isn't nearly as good as this one though.  The support available here is incredible for *buntu and was the prime reason I used Kubuntu for so long.  

The tipping point for me was Kubuntu Gutsy.  Unfortunately, it was just not cooperating on a bunch of things and felt slower than Feisty.  PCLOS just worked for me with no special configuration required, even my 7 button dual scrolling mouse!  And that was my deciding factor:  it was the only one that "just worked" for me.

About stability, PCLOS has been rock solid since I started using it and user switching actually works and doesn't require a random restart of X to get it going, like Kubuntu (Feisty and Gutsy; I think it was something weird with the nvidia drivers...)  

My .02

---

### Post by encho on 2007-11-17
As for being faster or more stable, that's all individual.

I have been using pclos for couple of months at my work (its network printer detection and configuration amazed me). But it was showstopper for me when latest init packages were out, which broke my raid. At home, latest nvidia drivers caused frequent freezes.

Than I installed latest Gutsy, and it felt much faster than pclos! And very, very stable. And I love Dolphin as default, it's kinda getting us ready for kde4. And synaptic is much faster.*And... well, as I said, it is matter of preference. Will keep an eye on pclos however.

Think kubuntu could learn a lot in terms of configuration (there is even grub and splash screen conf and kubuntu is sticking with plain black grub and unconfigurable progress bar and logo). Also software suggesting part of the forum, I just love it.

### Post by Brian96 on 2007-12-13
> **GSF1200S said:**
> Try the LiveCD.. thats the best way to get an idea or feel for it. It has its own control center which seems like itd be useful. Im more comfortable using the terminal for most stuff though, so I didnt give it a fair shake. 

I loved all the principles of PCLos- KDE, uses apt (4rpm), strives to be stable, etc.. but it ran like crap on my machine. Wireless wouldnt work unless I recompiled the kernel, sound wouldnt work, it wouldnt shutdown properly and it was slower than Buntu. That is similar to my experience. I like what PCLOS aspires to be--an exceptionally user and newbie-friendly free OS. The control center is exceptional, and I, too, hope to see Ubuntu move in a similar direction eventually.

The downsides, though, were that PCLOS was not nearly as mature for me in picking up hardware configurations. For example, on my laptop Ubuntu picks up the extra volume buttons, etc., without configuration, and PCLOS did not. Also, I found the PCLOS repos to be somewhat lacking, although I'm sure there are ways to deal with that. In particular, the Gnome options were really lacking (and it seems much easier to play around with different WMs and DEs in Ubuntu).

### Post by jrusso2 on 2007-12-28
PcLinuxOS has a smaller repository and a smaller community.  But it includes a lot of "non-free" drivers and codecs that make getting it up and running easier.

Ubuntu has a large community and has a large amount of software available for it.  Its kept up to date and patched.

Gnome vs kde is a personal choice

KDE is a lot more configurable while gnome is very basic and simple.

### Post by bobpress on 2007-12-29
> **ElEdwards said:**
> FYI, there is now a Gnome version of PCLinuxOS, which is what I'm running right now.
Here's the link:
[PCLOS Gnome 2.21.2]("http://www.linuxgator.org/Gnome/gnome_page/gnome.html")

I use Ubuntu for my laptop and use PCLinuxOS on a USB drive. Having various Linux distributions is a strength, since we have a lot of choices.  It gets to be a weakness when it leads to disagreement about 'which distro is best'.  If Ubuntu is your first Linux distribution, you should try another if it does not seem to meet your needs.

PCLinuxOS uses KDE instead of the Gnome desktop.  If you already have PCLinuxOS and want Gnome, try this link:
[Installing GNOME On PCLinuxOS 2007]("http://www.howtoforge.com/pclinuxos_gnome")
for information on how to add Gnome to the default KDE, just as you can add Gnome to Kubuntu or KDE to Ubuntu.  You will need to also install gksu while in KDE to be able to use Synaptic in Gnome desktop if you follow their directions.

### Post by Antman on 2008-01-27
> **67GTA said:**
> I have yet to try that one. What apps come with the mini edition? Are the multimedia extras still included(flash,java,codecs)?

The mini cd doesn't come with any office apps, codecs, or multimedia players. It basically is a VERY minimal KDE setup. It allows you to install (via Synaptic) only the apps you want/need. Once done you can create an .iso of your system. You can even use the USBlive utility to make an usb flash of your iso. It's pretty slick.

### Post by Hallvor on 2008-03-06
1. Yes.
2. Very nice. It uses KDE, but you can tweak it to look like Gnome if you want to. I am addicted to two panels.
3. Pros: It boots very fast, uses little memory and has a lot of options (with KDE). It is also a rolling release, so that you get the newest software. The Pclos (Mandriva) Control Center is also very nice, and the backup solution mkdistro is brilliant.
Cons: Smaller community, bad localization support.

It is all a matter of taste, but I prefer PCLinuxOS to regular Ubuntu. Am kind of drawn between Xubuntu and PCLinuxOS, though. They are IMHO both very, very good.

### Post by ljpp on 2008-03-16
Come on, don't turn this into a yet another distro bashing thread. The point isn't the pre-installed codecs or live installers (Ubuntu has a damn good one as well).

The real beef in the story is the 6 months release cycle vs. slower cycle and rolling updates. During early developement phases such "release early, release often"-strategies are valid, but I think major Linux distros have really reached such a high level in usability and functionality, that the focus should turn to maturizing and finalizing the releases. Slowing down the release cycle, produce more mature releases and longer life cycles via rolling updates.

Let's take a non-Ubuntu example (to keep people calm and stay on topic), let's look at Mandriva. Their 2008 and 2008.1 are AWSOME, but are only supported for about a year - even the powerpack! They look damn good and provide "windows like" experience, that would be easy to sell for small/home office as windows replacement, but not with that kind of life-cycle. Ubuntu at least has the LTS releases (but lack desktop polish).

---

This one thing is the downfall of Windows as a whole and will ruin Microsoft in the future because I don't believe that allowing a user to log in as an administrator and stay on as administrator is healthy for any network or individual PC.

### Post by Brian96 on 2008-03-21

Can someone please explain to me the difference between an LTS edition of Ubuntu and the "rolling update" method PCLOS uses?

---

### Post by Perpetual on 2008-03-21
> **Brian96 said:**
> Can someone please explain to me the difference between an LTS edition of Ubuntu and the "rolling update" method PCLOS uses?

LTS is Long Term Support.  Ubuntu uses a Fixed Release schedule and every 6 months the new release is rolled out.  Every 18 months the new LTS is rolled out.  Hardy being the 2nd LTS.

PCLOS uses a rolling release schedule where updates to software and kernel are updated after they have been tested.  There is no fixed date for when updates will be released.  In theory, this keeps your system more up to date depending on the Devs and packagers, testing, etcetera.

---

### Post by AdamWill on 2008-03-21
frak: both the EUCD and DMCA specifically do not allow 'fair use' exemptions. The academic exemption of the DMCA is rather narrowly phrased, but it's true it might get an educational institution a pass for hosting dvdcss, I hadn't thought of that. In the EU it depends on each nation's specific implementation of the EUCD.

win32-codecs, though, can't get out on either of those clauses. It contains a bunch of files simply ripped straight out of Windows, Quicktime and other copyrighted applications, there's no fair use or educational exemption there.

---

### Post by AdamWill on 2008-03-21
Brian: to go beyond what Perpetual says: PCLOS doesn't provide support for a stable release, they essentially don't have stable releases. So it's not really an equivalent situation. A permanently rolling distro like PCLOS is what some people want - they want the latest software all the time. But it's not the same as an Ubuntu LTS release. In an Ubuntu LTS release, packages don't just get updated to new versions for the heck of it: it's a *stable* release which gets security updates and major bug fixes for a long period. Packages are updated only to fix particular types of problems. This increases the stability and 'predictability' of the system (you know it will do tomorrow pretty much the same as what it does today, which is very valuable to *other* people, and not what a permanently rolling distribution does at all).

---

### Post by Brian96 on 2008-03-22
> **AdamWill said:**
> Brian: to go beyond what Perpetual says: PCLOS doesn't provide support for a stable release, they essentially don't have stable releases. So it's not really an equivalent situation. A permanently rolling distro like PCLOS is what some people want - they want the latest software all the time. But it's not the same as an Ubuntu LTS release. In an Ubuntu LTS release, packages don't just get updated to new versions for the heck of it: it's a *stable* release which gets security updates and major bug fixes for a long period. Packages are updated only to fix particular types of problems. This increases the stability and 'predictability' of the system (you know it will do tomorrow pretty much the same as what it does today, which is very valuable to *other* people, and not what a permanently rolling distribution does at all).

Thanks for the additional clarification. Now that I understand the difference, I'm not really sure what category I fall into. I think I'm somewhere in between.

For many of the applications I use--Thunderbird, Firefox, OO.o--I do want the latest stable version (and I don't want to have to compile from source to get it). But I don't necessarily want the latest drivers, kernel, or other "behind the scenes" stuff, unless it greatly enhances functionality. On the other hand, although I haven't even been using Ubuntu for a full year yet, I'm a little tired of a complete upgrade every 6 months (although my interest in this comes and goes).

I may give the newer PCLOS Gnome version a try. I got turned off of PCLOS because some random program that I use wasn't in the repos, and that made me wonder what ELSE wasn't in their repos. But maybe a rolling release is more up my alley.

In that case, you may want to go with Debian and upgrade to its Testing branch. It's not as easy to manage (no control center), but you should be able to keep an install and simply dist-upgrade for a few years and you'll learn something about how Debian works (which in turn shows how Ubuntu works). Not that I'm saying PCLOS is bad, but Debian has a far more rigorous testing method for its packages.

---

### Post by AdamWill on 2008-03-22
kingsley: no, Ubuntu would have overtaken PCLOS today regardless, if you look at the trends of the numbers over the last few weeks. If you look on the shorter timeframes (a month or a week), Ubuntu is way higher.

Brian96: it sounds, indeed, like you want something in the middle. I suspect you'd be best off with a stable distribution that is very easily upgradeable and has available repositories (whether third party or official) to provide the newer packages you want right away. Unfortunately, as far as I'm aware, such a thing doesn't quite exist. :) Both Ubuntu and Mandriva (and, I believe, OpenSUSE, though I'm less familiar with that) are distros that work on the stable release system and have repositories available where you could get updated versions of specific applications, as you want. However, if I'm being honest, neither is guaranteed to give you a smooth ride when upgrading to the next version; from what I've seen, a minority, but a significant minority, of users who try to upgrade from one Ubuntu release to the next or one Mandriva release to the next have problems. Again I'm less familiar with OpenSUSE, but I believe the situation is similar there (it's extremely, extremely hard to build a 'stable release' distribution and guarantee that upgrades will be trouble-free). So whichever way you go you're going to wind up with a compromise. Perhaps you might actually be best off running Debian sid or testing.

---
